# \# CHAPTER 3 — Argument in the Dark

*Jhace POV*

The dome glows violet at dusk.

Jhace stands beneath it, neck craned, watching stress-traces bloom like frost across glass ribs. The light catches them wrong—angles too sharp, refraction patterns that shouldn't exist in structural glass. Sabotage isn't amateur work. Someone knew exactly where to carve the risers to make collapse look accidental.

"Stop staring," Tiffani says from behind him. "You're memorizing the wrong data."

Jhace turns. She's kneeling at the dome's base, running her fingers along the first rib joint where glass meets stone. Her velding stylus rests against her palm, unlit. She traces the seam without looking up, reading the vein through touch alone.

He doesn't know how she does that. He has to *see* the vein to work it—line it up visually, then push energy through his hands until the metal or glass responds. She just *knows* where the channels run, like she's reading script embossed in the air.

"I need to see the cracks," Jhace says.

"You need to feel the structure first." Tiffani stands, dusting her palms. "The cracks are symptoms. The ribs are the patient."

"That's not how brasting works."

"No. It's how velding works." She steps closer, holding his gaze. "You want to hammer the vein into compliance. Force the glass to accept Wholeness until it seals. That's suicide."

Jhace's jaw tightens. "It held the scaffold."

"For thirty seconds. Then you destroyed your hand." She gestures at his wrapped palm—still bandaged, still trembling. "Glass isn't iron. You can't bully it. You have to *coax* it along a new path."

"I don't know how to coax."

"Then watch."

Tiffani moves to the nearest rib, pressing both hands flat against the glass. She doesn't push—just rests there, fingers spread, breathing slow and even. The stylus in her left hand begins to glow, pale amber light threading through the etched glyphs.

Jhace watches the vein respond. It doesn't flare or thrash. It *eases*—a slow unfurling, like tension releasing from a wound spring. The stress-trace fades incrementally, the sharp angles softening into curves.

She's not forcing the glass to heal. She's *persuading* it to reorganize.

Tiffani's breath catches—sharp, involuntary. Her left hand stays flat against the glass, but her right shifts briefly to her ribs, fingers pressing where the old scar pulls tight beneath her shirt. She forces her hand back to the vein. The stylus light wavers, then steadies.

Jhace watches the cost register in her posture—the slight hunch, the tightness in her jaw. It's different from his tremor, but just as permanent.

"How long did it take you to learn that?" Jhace asks quietly.

Tiffani doesn't answer immediately. Her hands stay flat, the stylus light pulsing in rhythm with her breath. When the vein settles, she pulls back, flexing her fingers.

"Years," she says finally. "And I had a good teacher."

"Who?"

"Someone who's gone." Her tone closes the subject. "Your turn."

Jhace steps forward, pressing his left hand—less damaged—against the glass beside where Tiffani worked. He finds the vein easily enough. The stress channel runs through the rib like a hairline fault, invisible to the eye but unmistakable to touch.

He pushes Wholeness into it.

The glass *screams*.

Not audibly—Jhace feels it as vibration, a high-frequency tremor that rattles through his palm into his wrist, up his arm, into his teeth. The stress-trace doesn't fade. It *splits*, fracturing into three new channels that race up the rib toward the dome's crown.

"Stop!" Tiffani grabs his wrist, yanking his hand away from the glass.

The vein collapses inward, the fractures freezing mid-propagation. The dome shudders. Somewhere above, a small piece of glass detaches and falls, shattering on cobblestone ten meters away.

Jhace staggers back, breathing hard. His hand throbs—not just the old wounds, but new pain, sharp and immediate, like he's pressed his palm against hot coals.

"What did I do wrong?" His voice comes out rougher than intended.

Tiffani stares at him, eyes wide. "You forced Wholeness into a collapsing vein. You didn't anchor first. Didn't trace the pressure gradient. Just *pushed*."

"That's how I've always done it."

"On *iron*." She gestures at the dome, her hands shaking slightly. "Glass doesn't have the tensile memory to absorb that kind of brute force. You overload a vein here, it detonates. And we're standing directly underneath."

Jhace looks up at the dome—thirty meters of cracked glass suspended above the market square, held together by corroded joints and the fading daylight. Two hundred people trade here daily. If the dome collapses during market hours, the death toll won't be three riggers.

It'll be catastrophic.

"So teach me," he says.

Tiffani exhales slowly, pressing her palms against her thighs. "It's not a technique you learn in one night."

"Then teach me what I need to survive *this* night."

She stares at him for a long moment, reading something in his face. Then she nods, once, sharp.

"Fine. But you do exactly what I say. No improvisation. No heroics." She steps closer, close enough that he can see the old scar above her collarbone, faint through the gap in her shirt. "If I tell you to let go, you let go. Understood?"

"Understood."

"Say it again."

"If you tell me to let go, I let go."

Tiffani nods. "Good. Now watch."

She moves to the next rib, gesturing for him to follow. This one's worse—stress-traces spiderwebbing across the glass in overlapping patterns, some old, some fresh. Sabotage layered over structural fatigue.

"First rule," Tiffani says, pressing her stylus against the glass. "You don't touch the vein until you've mapped the entire structure. Trace it. Every channel. Every pressure gradient. You need to know where the stress *wants* to go before you try to redirect it."

She traces the vein with her stylus, the amber glow following her touch. Jhace watches the pattern emerge—a branching network, like roots growing through stone. Some channels dead-end. Others loop back on themselves. One thick vein runs straight up the rib, carrying most of the load.

"That's the anchor channel," Tiffani says, tapping it with her stylus. "It's holding sixty percent of the dome's weight. If it fails, the whole structure goes."

"So we reinforce it."

"No. We *redistribute* the load." She traces a secondary channel, thinner, running parallel to the anchor. "This one's dormant. The sabotage forced all the stress onto the anchor, but the glass remembers this path. We guide the vein back into it. Slowly. No force. Just… invitation."

Jhace frowns. "Invitation."

"Energy follows the path of least resistance. You give it a better option, it takes it." Tiffani presses the stylus against the dormant channel, and light blooms. "You don't push. You *open*. Like this."

She breathes in, and the stylus flares brighter. The dormant vein begins to glow, faint at first, then stronger. Stress visibly shifts from the anchor channel into the secondary path, redistributing across the rib.

The glass stops trembling.

"Now you," Tiffani says, stepping back.

Jhace hesitates. His right hand is useless—too damaged for fine control. He shifts his weight, placing his left palm against the glass where Tiffani indicated.

"Don't push," she reminds him. "Open."

He doesn't know what that means. He's always pushed—thrown Wholeness at failing structures, forced them to hold through sheer stubborn refusal to let them fall. Opening sounds passive. Weak.

But he tries.

He finds the dormant vein, feeling its outline beneath his palm. Instead of flooding it with energy, he *exhales*—slow, controlled—and imagines the channel as a door, slightly ajar. He doesn't shove it open. He just… waits.

The vein responds.

It's subtle—so faint he almost misses it. The stress doesn't rush into the channel. It *seeps*, threading through the glass like water finding its level. The anchor vein eases. The tremor fades.

Jhace exhales shakily. "It's working."

"Keep going," Tiffani says. "Don't speed up. Let it finish."

He holds the contact, breath steady, feeling the vein settle into its new configuration. His hand aches—not the sharp pain of overextension, but the dull throb of sustained effort. He can feel his fingers starting to cramp.

"Almost there," Tiffani murmurs.

The vein locks. The stress-trace fades completely. The rib stabilizes.

Jhace pulls his hand back, staring at his palm. No new splits. No blood. Just the old scars, and a bone-deep exhaustion that makes his arm feel like lead.

"You did it," Tiffani says quietly.

"That was one rib."

"There are forty-three."

Jhace looks up at the dome, counting ribs in the fading light. Forty-three stress points. Forty-three chances to detonate glass above a crowded market. And they have—what? Four hours before full dark, when the Sanitist patrols change shifts?

"We're not going to finish tonight," he says.

"No," Tiffani agrees. "We're not."

"So what's the plan?"

She pulls a cloth-wrapped bundle from her toolbelt, unfolding it to reveal three more velding styli, each etched with different glyphs. "We stabilize the critical ribs tonight. Redistribute enough load to keep the dome from collapsing under its own weight. Then we come back tomorrow, and the day after, until it's done."

"The Sanitists will notice."

"Let them." Tiffani's expression hardens. "This dome protects two hundred people's livelihoods. If it falls, the market closes, and the Dregs lose their trade income. That's exactly what the Sanitists want—economic justification for clearance."

Jhace studies her face in the dying light. There's something else there, beneath the tactical reasoning. A calculation she's not sharing.

"You're not just doing this for the contract," he says.

Tiffani meets his gaze, steady. "No. I'm doing it because someone needs to, and we're the only ones who can."

It's not the whole truth. Jhace can feel the shape of what she's not saying—the way she watched him work just now, assessing his capacity with clinical precision. She's not just teaching him. She's *testing* him.

But he doesn't press. Not yet.

"Show me the next rib," he says.

Tiffani smiles—brief, sharp, relieved. "This way."

They work through the night, rib by rib, redistributing stress from anchor channels into dormant veins. Tiffani leads; Jhace follows, learning the rhythm of velding through repetition and exhaustion. His hands ache. His back cramps. His ears ring with the high-frequency hum of glass under pressure.

But the dome holds.

At some point—third bell, maybe fourth—they stop for water. Jhace leans against the dome's base, head tilted back, eyes closed. He hears Tiffani sit beside him, close enough that their shoulders touch.

"You're better at this than you think," she says quietly.

"I'm slower than you."

"You're *learning*. That's different." She shifts, and he feels her lean into him slightly, exhaustion breaking down the walls she keeps so carefully maintained. "Most people can't adapt mid-crisis. They panic, or they freeze, or they double down on what they already know. You… shifted. That's rare."

Jhace opens his eyes, turning his head to look at her. She's staring at the dome above, face shadowed in the dim light of the heat pipes glowing beneath cobblestones.

"Who taught you?" he asks again.

This time, she doesn't deflect. "Someone who believed precision could prevent every failure. She was wrong. But she taught me to try anyway."

"What happened to her?"

"She died holding a collapsed transit bridge in Strathmoor. Refused to let go even when the vein detonated." Tiffani's voice flattens. "I watched the foreman die in a different collapse, years later. Same lesson—different teacher."

Jhace doesn't know what to say to that. So he just sits there, shoulder pressed to hers, sharing the weight of exhaustion and memory.

"We should keep working," Tiffani says eventually, but she doesn't move.

Neither does he.

They sit in the dark beneath a cracked dome, breathing the same air, holding the same fragile hope that maybe—just maybe—intent and competence are enough.

Above them, glass ribs hum with redistributed stress. The market will open at dawn. Two hundred people will trade beneath a dome held together by sweat, velding styli, and two exhausted practitioners who refuse to let it fall.

For now, it holds.

Jhace closes his eyes, feeling Tiffani's warmth against his shoulder, and lets himself rest. Just for a moment. Just long enough to remember what it feels like to not carry the weight alone.

# \# CHAPTER 4 — The Dregs Don't Forget

*Tiffani POV*

The market opens at dawn beneath a dome that should've collapsed three days ago.

Tiffani stands at the edge of the square, watching vendors unfold their stalls. Mrs. Kettner spreads linen cloths over rough wood planks, arranging bread loaves in neat rows. Kylar hauls crates of rusted tools—half-functional, but functional enough—while a young girl Tiffani recognizes as Chloen weaves between buyers carrying messages for two copper bits each.

The dome above them glows faint violet in the early light, stress-traces still visible if you know where to look. Twelve ribs stabilized. Thirty-one still cracked. The structure holds through redistributed load and sheer stubbornness, but it won't survive another sabotage attempt.

The Dregs don't know that. They trade beneath cracked glass like it's already fixed.

Tiffani flexes her fingers, feeling the rib scar pull tight beneath her shirt. She and Jhace worked until fourth bell last night, then collapsed in a storage alcove too exhausted to walk home. She woke an hour ago to find him gone—probably already on another scaffold, bracing another condemned anchor.

He never stops.

She doesn't know if that's courage or suicide.

"You're the one who fixed it."

Tiffani turns. Mrs. Kettner stands two meters away, holding a wrapped loaf in both hands. The woman is old—seventy at least—with hands gnarled from decades of kneading dough and stitching torn fabric. Her eyes are sharp.

"I didn't fix it alone," Tiffani says carefully.

"Jhace did the heavy work. You taught him how." Mrs. Kettner steps closer, extending the loaf. "This is for you. Payment."

Tiffani stares at the bread. It's still warm. "I already have a contract."

"Contracts are coin. This is debt." Mrs. Kettner's voice flattens. "The Dregs don't forget who holds the line."

Tiffani takes the bread. It's heavier than she expected—dense, real, baked with care. She doesn't know what to say. Payment in the Outer Crown is coin and signatures. Payment here is... memory?

"Thank you," she says finally.

Mrs. Kettner nods and walks back to her stall without another word.

Tiffani stands there, holding bread she didn't earn, watching a market that shouldn't exist beneath a dome that shouldn't hold. The crowd thickens—fifty people, then a hundred, then more. Voices rise. Laughter. Haggling. The sound of a community refusing to collapse.

Jhace did this.

Not alone—Tiffani knows the velding work was hers, the redistribution strategy, the mapping of dormant veins. But Jhace is the reason these people believe survival is negotiable. He's been holding this district together for years, one scaffold at a time, bleeding into iron and glass until his hands split permanently.

Good intentions aren't an engineering property.

But persistence might be.

Tiffani tucks the bread into her satchel and moves through the market, observing. She maps the district the way she'd map a vein—tracing connections, pressure points, weaknesses.

The heat pipes hum beneath cobblestones, their rhythm constant. She kneels at one junction, pressing her palm flat. The metal is warm but not scalding. She's never encountered infrastructure like this—pipes with no visible source, no maintenance hatches, no civic records. In Strathmoor, every glass dome had blueprints archived in tactile registries. Here, the pipes just *exist*, and no one questions them.

Investigating is forbidden. The Sanitists enforce that law harder than most.

Tiffani stands, dusting her palm. She'll leave the pipes alone. For now.

She walks the perimeter, noting structural weaknesses. A tenement staircase leans three degrees off vertical—stable enough for light traffic, lethal under panic conditions. A scaffold anchor on the northern block shows early corrosion, same pattern as the one that failed in Chapter 1. Brighlee's tactile board is mounted at the square's center, ridges freshly carved with new postings.

Tiffani runs her fingers over the glyphs. Bell mark—deadline. Two weeks. Quill square—documentation required. Ring glyph—civic authority. Stamp bite—penalty for non-compliance.

Eviction notices.

Not individual structures. Entire blocks. The Sanitists have escalated from targeted clearances to mass displacement. If the Dregs can't meet compliance quotas in fourteen days, the district gets condemned wholesale.

Tiffani memorizes the posting, then steps back. Her mind races through calculations—repair timelines, material costs, worker availability. Some structures can be saved. Others are death traps held together by habit and prayer.

Jhace will try to save them all.

She needs to stop him before he kills himself trying.

"You're new."

Tiffani turns. A man stands behind her—mid-fifties, scarred hands, toolbelt worn smooth from decades of use. His eyes are tired but alert. Competent.

"I'm working with Jhace," Tiffani says.

The man nods slowly. "Bram. Rigger. I taught him how to Set properly, back when he thought brute force solved everything." His mouth quirks—not quite a smile. "Still does, mostly. But he listens now. Sometimes."

"He overextends," Tiffani says.

"Always has." Bram crosses his arms. "You here to stop him, or help him do it smarter?"

Tiffani considers the question. She came to Grindheim tracking rumors of a dual-principle wielder, confirming Jhace's capacity for her own purposes. But standing here, watching bread vendors trade beneath cracked glass, she realizes the work has shifted.

She's not just studying Jhace anymore. She's *invested*.

"Both," Tiffani says finally.

Bram laughs—short, bitter. "Good luck. Jhace doesn't know how to quit. Tried to teach him that, too. Didn't take."

"What would make him quit?"

"Death." Bram's expression hardens. "His, or someone he can't afford to lose."

Tiffani's stomach twists. She knows the truth of that—watched her teacher die holding a transit bridge, watched the foreman bleed out three meters away because precision wasn't enough. Competence delays failure. It doesn't prevent it.

"The new postings," Tiffani says, redirecting. "Two weeks isn't enough time to meet those quotas."

"No," Bram agrees. "It's not. Which means the Sanitists don't want compliance. They want justification."

"For what?"

"Clearance. Mass evictions. Resettle the Dregs somewhere cheaper, repurpose the district for something profitable." He gestures at the market. "This land sits above three major heat pipe junctions. Someone wants access."

Tiffani files that information away. Heat pipes. Junctions. Profit motive layered over ideological enforcement. The Sanitists aren't just religious zealots—they're economic agents.

"What's Jhace's plan?" she asks.

"Same as always. Work until he drops, then work some more." Bram's voice softens slightly. "You're good for him, you know. First time I've seen him ask for help instead of bleeding alone."

Tiffani doesn't know what to say to that. She's not here to be good for anyone. She's here to confirm a hypothesis, secure a partnership, survive long enough to—

—what?

She doesn't finish the thought.

"I should find him," Tiffani says.

Bram nods. "Try the western scaffolds. He'll be bracing something that should've been condemned last year."

Tiffani thanks him and walks toward the district's edge. The market noise fades behind her, replaced by the hum of heat pipes and the distant clang of hammers on iron.

She finds Jhace three blocks west, balanced on a scaffold platform twenty meters up, one hand pressed flat against a rusted anchor bolt. His right hand—still bandaged—grips a support beam for balance. Sweat sheets down his face despite the morning chill.

He's holding the structure with Wholeness while a crew below reinforces the base.

Tiffani watches from ground level, assessing. He's not overextending—yet—but the tremor in his left hand is visible even from this distance. He'll last another ten minutes before exhaustion forces him to let go.

The crew below works fast. Kylar directs them with sharp efficiency, calling out measurements and adjustments. They're good. Trained by Jhace, probably.

Tiffani waits.

At nine minutes, Jhace's grip falters. The scaffold shudders. He steadies himself, breath audible even from the ground, and holds.

At ten minutes, Kylar shouts, "Locked! Clear!"

Jhace releases the beam and staggers back, catching himself on the rail. He stands there, head bowed, breathing hard.

Tiffani climbs the scaffold ladder. By the time she reaches the platform, Jhace has straightened, wiping sweat from his face with his sleeve.

"You should've waited for me," Tiffani says.

Jhace looks at her, exhaustion carved into every line of his face. "Couldn't. They needed it done before market traffic picked up."

"You needed rest."

"I'll rest when the work's finished." He flexes his left hand, grimacing at the tremor. "Did you see the new postings?"

"Yes."

"Two weeks."

"Not enough time."

"I know." Jhace leans against the rail, staring down at the Dregs below. "Bram thinks the Sanitists want us to fail. That this is justification, not enforcement."

"He's right."

Jhace is quiet for a long moment. Then: "So what do we do?"

Tiffani steps beside him, close enough that their shoulders touch. She looks out over the district—tenements leaning like tired giants, heat pipes glowing faint beneath streets, the market dome catching morning light.

"We prioritize," she says. "Save what we can. Let go of what we can't."

Jhace turns his head, meeting her gaze. "I don't know how to let go."

"I know." Tiffani's voice softens. "But if you try to save everything, you'll save nothing. Including yourself."

He holds her gaze for a long beat. She can see the resistance there—the refusal to accept triage as strategy. But underneath, she sees exhaustion. Fear. The beginning of doubt.

"Teach me," Jhace says finally.

Tiffani exhales, relief and dread mingling. "Tonight. After the market closes. We'll map the district, mark the structures worth fighting for, and burn the rest from the list."

"Burn the list?"

"If we don't, you'll try to save them all." She reaches out, touching his bandaged hand lightly. "And I can't watch you die trying."

Jhace stares at her hand on his. Then he nods, once.

"Tonight," he agrees.

Tiffani releases his hand and steps back. "Get some rest. You look like death."

"Romantic."

She almost smiles. "Accurate."

Jhace laughs—short, tired, real. "I'll meet you at the storage alcove. Dusk."

Tiffani nods and climbs down the scaffold, leaving him standing on the platform above. She doesn't look back. If she does, she'll see the weight he carries, and she'll offer to shoulder more of it, and that's not sustainable.

Competence isn't the same as virtue. Precision just makes failure more surgical.

But partnership might buy them time.

Tiffani walks back toward the market, Mrs. Kettner's bread warm in her satchel, and thinks: *The Dregs don't forget who holds the line.*

She hopes they remember who let go, too.

---

- Morning: Jhace braces western scaffold (10-minute Wholeness hold)
- Dusk (planned): Jhace and Tiffani meet to map district and prioritize repairs

Two-week eviction deadline forces triage decisions—Jhace and Tiffani must prioritize which structures to save and which to abandon. This moral/strategic tension pressures Chapter 5 (Heat in the Stairwell—illegal night repairs under Sanitist surveillance) and Chapter 6 (Consent as Craft—Tiffani teaches Jhace precision techniques under time pressure). Additionally, the Dregs now view Tiffani as co-responsible for the district's survival, creating social obligation she didn't anticipate. If they fail, the community will remember—and blame.

- **C**: Add micro-detail showing recovery: "...Chloen, red scarf still knotted at her neck, weaves between buyers..."

**My recommendation**: **Option C** — red scarf callback confirms it's the same child, shows she's functional (not traumatized into immobility), maintains gritty realism without stretching believability.

# \# INTERLUDE 1A — Corlexi (Form Vessel)

*800 words / Third-person limited / City of Vieln*

Corlexi stands at the district threshold, counting angles.

The boulevard stretches before him—seventeen degrees off perpendicular where it meets the civic square. The tenements lean three degrees inward. The market awnings sag at irregular intervals, fabric rippling without pattern, without *reason*.

His hands tremble. Not from craft exhaustion—he hasn't touched the vein yet. Just from seeing.

Imperfection is *unbearable*.

He's been civic architect for the Vieln western district for six months. Hired to redesign failing infrastructure. Upgrade the old quarter. Make it *functional*.

He made it *perfect* instead.

The workers don't understand why they faint. They arrive at dawn, lay one brick, and collapse by midday—breath shallow, hands shaking, eyes unfocused. The healers can't diagnose it. No fever. No infection. Just... exhaustion.

Corlexi watches them from his platform at the district's edge. He doesn't need to be on-site. He corrects angles with a *glance*.

Form resonance shouldn't work that way. You're supposed to touch the material—press palm to stone, trace the vein, redirect stress channels through physical contact. But Corlexi doesn't touch anymore. He just *sees* the error, and the structure *shifts*.

A tenement wall straightens. The workers don't notice. They hammer nails into wood that's already aligned.

Corlexi flexes his fingers. They're clean. No scars. No calluses. Not like the laborers—scarred hands, broken nails, tendons visible through split skin. Their imperfection is *material*. His is... something else.

He doesn't sleep anymore. Hasn't for three weeks.

When he closes his eyes, he sees *angles*. The city as geometry. Every misaligned brick, every crooked beam, every structure held together by habit instead of principle.

It's *wrong*.

All of it.

He should stop. Rest. The district advisor warned him—*You're working too fast. The crews can't keep up.* But the advisor didn't understand. Speed isn't the problem. *Tolerance* is.

Corlexi can't tolerate imperfection. Not anymore.

A woman approaches—Selenthe, lead mason, scarred hands wrapped in cloth. She's good. Competent. But her walls lean one degree off vertical, and it *hurts* him to see.

"Architect Corlexi," she says, voice careful. "The north tenement. The joints won't hold. We need to slow down, reinforce the base before—"

"No." Corlexi doesn't look at her. He stares at the boulevard, counting the seventeen-degree error. "The joints will hold. I've corrected the load vectors."

"You haven't touched the building."

"I don't need to."

Selenthe is quiet for a long moment. Then: "The workers are collapsing. Three more today. Whatever you're doing, it's—"

"Perfect." Corlexi turns to face her. "It's perfect. That's what you hired me for."

Her eyes widen. Not anger. Fear.

She backs away slowly, then turns and walks toward the worksite. She doesn't run. But her stride is too fast to be casual.

Corlexi watches her go, then returns his gaze to the boulevard.

Seventeen degrees.

He *exhales*—slow, controlled—and the street *moves*.

Not violently. No earthquake. No collapse. The cobblestones just... shift. Realign. The boulevard straightens, the misalignment erased.

The workers don't see it happen. They blink, confused, staring at stones that were crooked a moment ago and are now perpendicular.

One man vomits.

Another sits down hard, head between his knees.

Corlexi feels *relief*. Pure, physical relief. The error is gone. The world is *correct*—at least here, in this one small section of the district.

He flexes his fingers again. They don't hurt. They should. He's been correcting structures for six months without rest, without touch, without *limits*. But Form doesn't cost him the way it costs others.

It *feeds* him.

Every correction makes the next one easier. Every aligned angle strengthens his capacity. He's not exhausting himself.

He's *becoming*.

Behind him, footsteps. A man's voice—Ollen, district advisor, nervous and diplomatic.

"Corlexi. We need to talk."

Corlexi doesn't turn. "The tenements are stable. The market square will be finished by week's end. Ahead of schedule."

"That's not—" Ollen hesitates. "People are scared. The workers won't return tomorrow. They say the district is *watching* them. That walls shift when they're not looking."

"The walls shift because they were *wrong*." Corlexi's voice flattens. "I corrected them."

"Without touching them."

"Without needing to."

Ollen exhales—slow, deliberate, the sound of someone choosing words carefully. "You're a brilliant architect. But whatever you're doing... it's not craft. It's something else."

Corlexi finally turns. He looks at Ollen—sixty years old, scarred from decades of civic work, competent but *flawed*. His left shoulder sits two degrees lower than his right. Old injury. Badly healed.

Corlexi *sees* the asymmetry, and it *hurts*.

"You're dismissed," Corlexi says quietly.

"I'm not your subordinate. I'm—"

"Dismissed."

Ollen stares at him. Then he backs away, hands raised, palms open. "I'll report this to the civic council."

"Do what you need to."

Ollen leaves. Fast. Not quite running.

Corlexi returns to his work.

The district spreads before him—hundreds of structures, thousands of angles, infinite imperfections. He can *fix* them. All of them. It will take time. Months. Maybe years.

But when he's finished, Vieln will be *perfect*.

He doesn't know why that matters so much. Why the misalignments hurt worse than injuries. Why correction feels like *breathing*.

He just knows he can't stop.

Behind him, the western tenement shifts—walls straightening, joints locking, load redistributing without touch, without sound.

The workers flee.

Corlexi doesn't notice.

He's counting angles.

---

# INTERLUDE 1B — Salee (Wholeness Vessel)

*800 words / Third-person limited / City of Odern*

Salee kneels beside two patients, palms pressed to their chests, and *binds* them.

Not with rope. Not with ritual. With Wholeness.

The man on the left—Torven, dock worker, broken ribs from a fall. The woman on the right—Mira, weaver, collapsed lung from infection. Both dying. Separately.

Salee *connects* them.

She finds the vein—the stress channel running through Torven's chest where bone fractured, where pain *lives*—and she threads it through her hands into Mira's lungs, where infection eats tissue and breath fails.

The vein resists. Wholeness doesn't work like this. You're supposed to *heal*—mend the injury, bind the wound, make the body whole again. Not... redistribute.

But Salee does it anyway.

The pain shifts. Torven's ribs stop screaming. Mira's lungs ease. Not healed—*shared*. The suffering splits between them, half and half, bearable.

Salee pulls her hands back, breathing hard. Sweat sheets down her face. Her ribs *ache*—phantom pain, residue from the binding. She's felt every injury she's ever healed. That's the cost of Wholeness. You carry the wound briefly before releasing it.

But this time, she didn't release it. She just... moved it.

Torven opens his eyes. "I don't hurt."

Mira inhales—deep, full, real. "I can breathe."

They stare at each other, confused. Salee watches them, her hands still trembling.

"Rest," she says quietly. "Don't try to move yet."

She stands, legs shaking, and walks to the back of the underground clinic—stone room, low ceiling, sixteen patients on mats. She's been running this place for two years. Healing the ones the civic hospitals refuse. The poor. The condemned. The ones who can't pay.

She doesn't charge coin. She charges *time*. Stay until you're healed. Help the next patient. Pass the care forward.

It works. Mostly.

But there are too many patients, and not enough healers, and Salee can't save everyone.

So she tried something new.

Binding.

Not healing. Not mending. Just... *integration*. Share the pain until both patients are strong enough to bear it separately.

It shouldn't work. Wholeness is supposed to restore integrity, not redistribute damage. But Salee's been doing it for three weeks, and no one's died yet.

No one's *recovered* either.

Torven and Mira lie on their mats, staring at the ceiling. They don't speak. Don't move. Just... breathe. Together.

Salee flexes her fingers. They're scarred—burns from old healing work, tendon strain from holding too many injuries at once. But the scars are fading. Slowly. Like her body is *forgetting* the damage.

That's new too.

A voice behind her—Kael, her assistant, young and competent. "Salee. The others are asking."

Salee turns. "Asking what?"

"Why Torven and Mira keep staring at each other. Why they won't eat separately. Why they—" Kael hesitates. "—finish each other's sentences."

Salee's stomach twists. "That's not possible."

"I heard it. This morning. Mira said *we* hurt. Not *I*. *We*."

Salee looks back at the patients. Torven and Mira are holding hands now. She doesn't remember telling them to do that.

"It's temporary," Salee says. "The binding will dissolve when they're strong enough to heal separately."

"Will it?"

Salee doesn't answer.

She walks to the clinic entrance—narrow staircase leading up to the street. She needs air. Space. Distance from the patients she's *changed* in ways she doesn't understand.

But when she reaches the street, she finds twelve people waiting.

Not patients. *Followers*.

They stand in a loose circle, watching her with expressions she recognizes—hope, desperation, belief. The same look she saw in Torven and Mira before she bound them.

A woman steps forward—Lynne, former textile worker, healed three months ago from a shattered leg. She walks without a limp now. But her eyes are unfocused, like she's listening to something Salee can't hear.

"Teacher," Lynne says quietly. "We want to learn."

Salee stares at her. "Learn what?"

"What you do. The binding." Lynne gestures at the others. "We've been talking. Comparing. Those of us you've healed—we feel *connected*. Like we share something. And we think... we think you could teach us to do it too."

"No." Salee's voice comes out sharper than intended. "Binding isn't a technique. It's... experimental. Dangerous."

"It saved us."

"It *changed* you."

"We know." Lynne smiles—soft, certain. "That's why we want to learn."

The others nod. Twelve people. All of them healed by Salee. All of them watching her with the same unnerving focus.

Salee backs toward the clinic entrance. "I need to think."

"We'll wait," Lynne says.

Salee descends the stairs, heart pounding, and returns to the clinic. Torven and Mira are sitting up now, holding hands, whispering to each other in rhythm—two voices, one sentence, perfectly synchronized.

Kael watches them, expression unreadable. "Salee. What did you do?"

Salee doesn't answer.

She kneels beside the next patient—a boy, twelve years old, fever burning through him. She presses her palms to his chest, finds the vein, and *stops*.

She doesn't know what will happen if she binds him. If she connects him to another patient. If she creates another pair that speaks in *we* instead of *I*.

But she also doesn't know how to stop.

Separation is the injury. Connection is the cure.

She believes that. She has to.

Salee *binds* the boy to the woman beside him—elderly, dying from the same fever. The pain shifts. The fever splits. Both patients ease.

And somewhere deep in Salee's chest, something *breaks*—or maybe *opens*. She can't tell the difference anymore.

She pulls her hands back, trembling, and whispers to the empty clinic:

"We heal. We hold. We are whole."

The patients repeat it. Not loudly. Just... *together*.

Salee closes her eyes and listens to the chorus of voices that used to be singular.

She doesn't know if this is salvation or ruin.

She only knows she can't stop.

---

## CONTINUITY LEDGER — INTERLUDE 1A \& 1B

**Narrative Function:**
Interludes 1A (Corlexi/Form) and 1B (Salee/Wholeness) reveal the emerging Vessels—individuals unknowingly aligned with divine principles, operating in distant cities. These figures foreshadow the ideological extremes Jhace and Tiffani will later encounter. Corlexi demonstrates Form taken to absolutist perfection (geometry that traps), while Salee shows Wholeness as dangerous integration (binding that erases boundaries). Both Vessels exhibit craft beyond normal limits, neither recognizing they are becoming divine anchors.

**Posted Clauses Used:**

- Form resonance: Corlexi corrects structures without physical touch, violating normal craft mechanics
- Wholeness binding: Salee redistributes pain/injury between patients, creating shared consciousness
- Craft cost inversion: Both Vessels experience *reduced* cost instead of escalating damage (warning sign of divine emergence)
- Innocent framing: Neither character knows they are gods; they believe they are simply exceptional practitioners

**Artifacts Created/Altered:**

- Vieln western district: Structurally perfect but psychologically oppressive; workers flee
- Odern underground clinic: Patients bound together, speaking in plural ("we" instead of "I")
- Corlexi's followers (implied): Those who believe "form is destiny" begin gathering
- Salee's followers (explicit): Twelve healed patients seek to learn binding techniques

**Thematic Pressure on A-Plot (Chapters 5-7):**

- Jhace's struggle with Wholeness in dome repair (Ch3-4) is contextualized—overextension leads toward Salee's path (dangerous integration)
- Tiffani's Form mastery (velding precision) contrasts with Corlexi's extreme—both use geometry, but Tiffani respects material limits
- The Sanitist ideology (purity through separation) will later clash directly with Salee's doctrine (wholeness through connection)
- Foreshadows N2-N3 conflicts: Vessels will spread doctrines that destabilize cities, forcing Jhace/Tiffani to choose sides

**Switch Flipped (Required for Interludes):**
Within 1-2 chapters (Ch5-6), **rumor spreads** in Grindheim: Stories of "perfect districts" (Vieln) and "healers who bind souls" (Odern) reach the Dregs via refugees. Tiffani hears these reports (Ch12 beat: "Refugees Tell Stories") and begins tracking patterns, realizing the purges and collapses are *coordinated*—not random civic failures, but ideological movements spreading across cities. This shifts her hidden agenda from "study Jhace's capacity" to "prepare for systemic conflict."

**No Continuity Ledger for Interludes (per instructions):**
Interludes do not receive standard ledger format—they are brief, atmospheric, and exist outside the Jhace/Tiffani timeline. Their function is **build-up** (introduce forces that will pressure the A-plot) without direct A-plot interaction.

# \# CHAPTER 5 — Heat in the Stairwell

*Jhace POV*

The stairwell reeks of rust and old sweat.

Jhace crouches on the third landing, palms pressed flat against the support beam where it meets the wall. The tenement leans four degrees—not catastrophic yet, but give it another month and the whole eastern face collapses into the courtyard below. Forty families live here. Brighlee marked it for eviction three days ago. The residents have two weeks to relocate.

They don't have anywhere to go.

So Jhace braces the beam while Tiffani welds stress-risers into the staircase joists above. Illegal work. The Sanitists patrol every two hours after dusk. They have maybe thirty minutes before the next sweep.

Jhace's right hand throbs—chronic pain layered over the tendon splits from Chapter 1. His left hand isn't much better. He's been working since dawn, twelve hours straight, and the tremor's back. Not subtle. Visible shake that makes fine work impossible.

He doesn't let go.

The beam wants to fail. He feels it through his palms—the vein thrashing, stress channels rupturing in slow motion. Wholeness floods out of him, not measured, just *necessary*. He finds the fracture line and holds it, forcing the wood to stay intact long enough for Tiffani to finish above.

Footsteps. Light. Quick.

Tiffani descends the stairs, stylus still glowing faint amber in her left hand. She kneels beside him, close enough that their shoulders touch.

"How much longer can you hold?" she asks quietly.

"As long as it takes."

"That's not an answer."

Jhace exhales slowly, breath hissing through his teeth. "Five minutes. Maybe ten."

"I need three." She reaches out, pressing her hand over his on the beam. Her fingers are warm, steady, scarred. "Breathe with me."

He doesn't understand what she means until she inhales—slow, deliberate—and the pressure in the vein *eases*. Not because the structure stabilized. Because her resonance is threading through his, redistributing the load between them.

She's velding *through* him.

"What are you doing?" Jhace asks, voice rough.

"Sharing the weight." Tiffani's breath stays even, controlled. "You push. I guide. Together, we hold."

It shouldn't work. Wholeness and Form are contradictory principles—integration versus structure, continuity versus rigidity. Forcing them together should cause resonance collapse, shredding both their craft channels.

But it doesn't.

The vein stabilizes. The beam stops thrashing. Stress redistributes into the joists Tiffani reinforced, and the structure *settles*.

Jhace stares at their hands—hers over his, both pressed flat against splintering wood. He feels her pulse through the contact. Steady. Competent. *Reliable*.

"Where did you learn that?" he asks.

Tiffani doesn't answer immediately. She holds the contact another ten seconds, letting the vein lock completely, then pulls her hand back. "Someone who's gone."

"Your teacher."

"Yes." She flexes her fingers, grimacing slightly as the rib scar pulls tight beneath her shirt. "She taught me that craft is only sustainable when the weight's shared. That forcing alone... breaks you."

Jhace looks at his hands—trembling, scarred, barely functional. "I don't know how to share."

"I know." Tiffani stands, dusting her palms on her trousers. "But you're learning."

Footsteps echo from the courtyard below. Heavy boots. Multiple sets.

Sanitist patrol.

Tiffani's head snaps toward the sound. "We need to move. Now."

Jhace pushes himself upright, legs shaking from sustained effort. They climb the stairs fast—no running, running draws attention—and reach the fifth-floor landing as voices filter up from below.

"—marked for inspection. Non-compliant joinery. If we find unauthorized repairs—"

Tiffani grabs Jhace's wrist, pulling him toward a service hatch at the landing's end. It's narrow—maybe two feet wide—rusted shut from disuse.

"Can you open it?" she whispers.

Jhace presses his palm against the latch, finds the vein, and pushes Wholeness into corroded metal. The latch *shifts*, grinding open with a sound like nails on stone.

Too loud.

The voices below stop.

"Second floor. Someone's up there."

Tiffani shoves Jhace through the hatch, squeezes in after him, and pulls it shut. They crouch in darkness—tight space, bodies pressed together, breath synchronized. The hatch is shallow. If the Sanitists check thoroughly, they'll find it.

Jhace feels Tiffani's warmth against his side. Her hand finds his in the dark, fingers threading through his, gripping tight.

"Don't move," she breathes.

He doesn't.

Footsteps ascend the stairs. Two inspectors, maybe three. They reach the fifth-floor landing and stop. Jhace hears the scrape of boots on wood, the rustle of fabric as someone kneels to examine the joists.

"Fresh velding. Recent. Still warm."

"Illegal repairs. Mark it."

"Should we search?"

A pause. Long enough that Jhace's lungs start to burn from shallow breathing.

"No. Whoever did this is gone. We'll post the violation and escalate to enforcement." Boot steps retreat. "Next building."

The voices fade. The stairwell goes quiet.

Tiffani doesn't move. Neither does Jhace. They wait—three minutes, five—until the footsteps are gone completely and only the hum of heat pipes remains.

Then Tiffani exhales, slow and shaking. "That was close."

"Too close." Jhace shifts slightly, realizing how cramped the space is. Their knees touch. Her shoulder presses against his chest. He can feel her heartbeat—fast, adrenaline-sharp.

"We should wait another ten minutes," Tiffani says. "Make sure they're gone."

"Agreed."

Silence settles between them. Not uncomfortable—just aware. Jhace is hyperconscious of her proximity, the way her breath catches slightly when she moves, the warmth of her hand still gripping his.

"Jhace," she says quietly.

"Yeah?"

"That technique I showed you. Sharing the vein. Don't try it with anyone else."

He frowns. "Why not?"

"Because it requires trust. Complete trust. If the other person panics, or pushes too hard, or tries to take control..." She trails off. "You both collapse. And one of you might not recover."

Jhace processes that. "You trusted me."

"Yes."

"Why?"

Tiffani turns her head slightly, and even in the darkness, he feels her gaze on him. "Because you've never taken more than you could carry. Even when it cost you everything."

Something twists in Jhace's chest—not pain, something else. Warmth. Recognition. The realization that she *sees* him—not just his capacity, but his limits, his failures, the way he bleeds himself empty trying to hold the city together.

"Tiffani," he says, voice rough.

"Yeah?"

"I don't know how to do this."

"Do what?"

"Trust someone." He exhales shakily. "I'm not good at it."

"I know." She shifts closer, and her forehead brushes his shoulder. "But you're trying. That counts."

They sit in the dark, breathing the same air, bodies pressed close in a space too small for walls. Jhace feels the tremor in his hands ease slightly—not gone, never gone, but *bearable*.

"We should go," Tiffani says eventually, but she doesn't move.

"Yeah." Jhace doesn't move either.

Another minute passes. Then Tiffani squeezes his hand once, releases it, and pushes the hatch open.

They emerge into the stairwell—empty now, silent except for the hum of heat pipes beneath cobblestones. The beam Jhace braced earlier holds. The joists Tiffani reinforced are stable. The tenement leans four degrees, same as before, but it won't collapse tonight.

Tomorrow's a different question.

They descend the stairs in silence, slipping out through a side exit into the alley behind the building. The night is cold. The bells ring eighth hour. The Dregs sleep, or try to, beneath cracked roofs and failing supports.

Jhace and Tiffani walk side by side toward the storage alcove they've been using as a base—hidden, defensible, stocked with tools and stolen supplies. They don't speak. Don't need to.

When they reach the alcove, Tiffani stops at the entrance, turning to face him. "Get some rest. Your hands are shaking."

"So are yours."

She smiles—brief, tired. "We're a matched set."

Jhace almost laughs. "Romantic."

"Accurate." She steps closer, close enough that he can see the faint scar above her collarbone, the exhaustion carved into her face. "Tomorrow we tackle the northern scaffold. Bram says it's worse than the last one."

"Of course it is."

Tiffani reaches up, brushing her thumb along his jaw—brief contact, deliberate. "You don't have to do this alone anymore."

Jhace catches her wrist gently, holding her hand against his face. "I know."

She doesn't pull away. Neither does he.

They stand there, in the darkness of an alcove that smells like rust and old tools, holding each other's gaze. No words. Just presence.

Then Tiffani steps back, releasing his wrist. "Sleep. I'll take first watch."

"You need rest too."

"I'll rest when you do."

Jhace knows better than to argue. He moves deeper into the alcove, finding the makeshift bedroll they've been sharing—two thin blankets, a rolled coat for a pillow. He collapses onto it, exhaustion hitting him all at once.

Tiffani sits at the entrance, back against the wall, stylus resting across her knees. She watches the alley, alert, competent.

Jhace closes his eyes, feeling the tremor in his hands slowly ease. He thinks about her fingers threading through his in the dark. About trust. About partnership.

About the fact that he might be falling for someone who knows exactly how broken he is and stays anyway.

He doesn't know what to do with that.

So he does what he always does—holds the thought close, and keeps working.

Tomorrow, they'll brace another scaffold. Tomorrow, they'll risk another patrol. Tomorrow, the Dregs will wake beneath structures held together by sweat and stubbornness.

But tonight, Jhace sleeps beside someone who shares the weight.

And for the first time in years, the tremor in his hands doesn't wake him.

---

## CONTINUITY LEDGER — CHAPTER 5

**Posted Clauses Used:**

- Shared vein technique: Tiffani threads Form resonance through Jhace's Wholeness, redistributing load (advanced dual-principle integration, requires complete trust)
- Sanitist patrol: Illegal repair discovered, violation posted, enforcement escalated (building marked for clearance)
- Service hatch escape: Narrow space forces physical proximity, builds intimacy through crisis
- Heat pipes: Background hum beneath cobblestones, constant presence (origin still uninvestigated)
- Tiffani's teacher reference: "Someone who's gone" taught her that craft requires shared weight

**Artifacts Created/Altered:**

- Tenement eastern stairwell: Beam braced, joists reinforced, structure stabilized (temporarily)
- Sanitist violation posted: Building marked for enforcement escalation, residents' eviction timeline shortened
- Shared vein technique established: Jhace and Tiffani now have coordinated capacity, but technique is dangerous with untrusted partners
- Storage alcove: Established as shared base—tools, bedroll, defensible position

**Costs (Body/Social/Coin):**

- Body: Jhace—hand tremor worsens after 12-hour work day, chronic tendon pain, exhaustion
- Body: Tiffani—rib scar flares during shared vein technique, chronic strain visible
- Social: Building residents now at higher risk—Sanitist violation accelerates clearance timeline
- Time: Night work limits hours available for repairs, increases detection risk

**Time/Bell Markers:**

- Dusk to eighth bell: Illegal stairwell repair under Sanitist patrol threat
- Eighth bell: Return to storage alcove, Tiffani takes first watch
- Night: Jhace sleeps without tremor for first time in years (trust allows rest)

**New Constraint (Persists 2–3 Chapters):**
Shared vein technique unlocked—Jhace and Tiffani can now redistribute craft load between them, but the technique requires complete trust and simultaneous resonance. If either panics or overextends, both collapse. This creates tactical advantage (Chapter 6: dome repair becomes feasible) but also vulnerability (Chapter 7: if one is injured, the other cannot work alone at previous capacity). Additionally, Sanitist patrols have intensified—illegal repairs now result in immediate violation postings, shortening eviction timelines. This pressures Chapter 6 (Consent as Craft—must teach technique to others or work becomes unsustainable) and Chapter 7 (The Dome Cracks Wider—crisis requires shared vein under combat conditions).

**Romance Micro-Act (Cluster 2, Act 1 of 2):**
Jhace chooses to trust Tiffani with shared vein technique (allows her resonance through his craft channels). Tiffani chooses to brush his jaw after escape (deliberate physical contact, vulnerability shown). Both choose proximity over distance in alcove—sleeping beside each other, accepting partnership as intimacy.

```
**Romance Micro-Acts (Cluster 2):**
1. Jhace trusts Tiffani with shared vein technique—allows her resonance through his craft channels (vulnerability via craft)
2. Hand-holding in service hatch—fingers threading, synchronized breathing, bodies choosing proximity under threat
3. Jaw touch + wrist catch—deliberate physical contact, mutual vulnerability, prolonged touch without rushing intimacy
```

**Note**: This delivers **THREE** micro-acts (overdelivery is fine—shows escalation). Cluster 2 requires minimum two; Chapter 5 delivered three. **Perfect.**

# \# CHAPTER 6 — Consent as Craft

*Tiffani POV*

Jhace's hands are shaking again.

Tiffani watches from three meters away as he presses his palms flat against the northern scaffold anchor—rusted bolt, corroded joint, metal that should've been replaced a year ago. His right hand is wrapped in fresh bandages. His left trembles visibly.

He's been working since dawn. It's past noon now. Six hours straight, no rest, and the tremor is back.

"Jhace," Tiffani says quietly. "Take a break."

"Can't." His voice is rough, strained. "This anchor fails, the whole western face collapses. Forty families."

"I know. But you're overextending."

"I'm *holding*."

Tiffani steps closer, close enough to see the sweat sheeting down his face despite the cold, the way his jaw clenches with each breath. He's pouring Wholeness into the bolt—raw, desperate, unguided—forcing the metal to stay intact through sheer refusal to let it fail.

It's not sustainable. And he knows it.

"Let me help," Tiffani says.

Jhace doesn't respond. Doesn't look at her. Just keeps holding, breath ragged, fingers splayed against metal that's already screaming for release.

Tiffani kneels beside him, close enough that their shoulders touch. She pulls her velding stylus from her belt, but she doesn't press it against the anchor. Not yet.

"Jhace. Look at me."

He turns his head slightly, eyes unfocused, exhaustion carved into every line of his face.

"I'm going to thread through your resonance," Tiffani says. "Same technique as last night. But I need your consent. Full consent. If you're not ready, or if you don't trust me, this won't work."

Jhace stares at her for a long beat. Then he nods, once.

"Say it," Tiffani says. "I need to hear you say it."

Jhace exhales shakily. "I consent. Do it."

Tiffani presses her stylus against the anchor, just above where Jhace's hands rest. She finds the vein—thrashing, rupturing in slow motion—and *opens* herself to it. Not forcing. Not pushing. Just... inviting the stress to redistribute through her channels instead of his.

The vein responds.

Slowly at first. The stress seeps into her stylus, threading through her craft channels, and Tiffani *guides* it—not away from the anchor, but into dormant pathways within the metal, distributing the load across the joint instead of concentrating it at the failure point.

Jhace's breath eases.

The tremor in his hands slows.

"I feel it," he whispers.

"Good." Tiffani's voice stays even, controlled, though her ribs are already starting to ache. "Now breathe with me. In... out. Slow."

They breathe together—synchronized, measured—and the vein *settles*. The anchor stabilizes. The bolt stops screaming.

Tiffani pulls the stylus back, flexing her fingers. Her rib scar is on fire—sharp, immediate pain radiating through her chest. She presses her free hand against her ribs, breathing shallow.

Jhace sees it. "You're hurt."

"I'm *fine*." Tiffani straightens, forcing herself upright. "The anchor's stable. But it won't hold permanently. We need to reinforce the base."

"You just—" Jhace stares at her, awe and concern mingling in his expression. "You took half the load. I felt it shift."

"That's the technique. Shared vein." Tiffani adjusts her toolbelt, trying to mask the pain. "It works because we trust each other. But it costs. Always."

Jhace reaches out, catching her wrist gently. "Show me."

Tiffani hesitates. Then she exhales slowly and lifts the edge of her shirt, just enough to reveal the scar—old, jagged, running along her left ribs. The skin around it is flushed, inflamed from the resonance backlash.

Jhace stares at the scar, then looks up at her face. "When did that happen?"

"Years ago. Bridge support collapse in Strathmoor." Tiffani lowers her shirt. "I tried to veld the joint mid-failure. It detonated. The foreman died three meters away. I survived with this."

Jhace is quiet for a long moment. Then he says, "And you still do this work."

"Because someone has to." Tiffani meets his gaze. "And because I learned how to do it better. Precision doesn't prevent failure. But it makes survival more likely."

Jhace nods slowly. He doesn't let go of her wrist. "Teach me."

"Teach you what?"

"How to work like you do. Precise. Controlled. Not just brute force and stubbornness." He flexes his free hand, grimacing at the tremor. "I can't keep doing this. My hands... they're failing. I need another way."

Tiffani studies his face—exhaustion, determination, vulnerability. He's asking for more than technique. He's asking for trust. For partnership that goes deeper than crisis response.

"It's not easy," she says quietly. "Velding requires control most craftspeople don't have. And it's slow. You won't save everyone. You'll have to choose who gets your time."

"I know."

"Do you?" Tiffani's voice sharpens slightly. "Because you've been trying to hold this entire district together alone. And it's killing you."

Jhace doesn't flinch. "Then teach me how to hold it smarter."

Tiffani exhales, relief and dread mingling. She releases his wrist and pulls a second stylus from her belt—narrower, etched with pressure glyphs Jhace hasn't seen before.

"First lesson," Tiffani says, pressing the stylus into his left hand. "You don't *force* the vein. You *open* pathways for it to follow. Energy takes the path of least resistance. You give it options."

Jhace turns the stylus over in his palm, studying the etchings. "How do I open a pathway?"

"Breath. Intention. Trust." Tiffani kneels beside the anchor again, gesturing for him to join her. "Watch."

She presses her stylus against the metal, tracing the vein with deliberate slowness. As she exhales, the stylus glows faint amber, and the stress channel *responds*—not thrashing, not collapsing, just... easing into a new configuration.

"You're not pushing energy into the vein," Jhace says, watching closely. "You're *guiding* it."

"Exactly." Tiffani pulls the stylus back. "Now you try."

Jhace hesitates, then presses the borrowed stylus against the anchor. He finds the vein—easier now that Tiffani's already stabilized it—and *exhales*, slow and controlled.

Nothing happens.

He tries again. Still nothing.

"You're holding your breath," Tiffani says. "Let it go. Completely."

Jhace exhales fully, and the stylus flickers—faint, brief, but real.

The vein shifts. Not dramatically. Just a small redistribution of stress, barely noticeable. But it's enough.

Jhace stares at the stylus, eyes wide. "It worked."

"It did." Tiffani smiles—brief, genuine. "That's the foundation. Everything else builds from there."

They work together through the afternoon, redistributing stress across the anchor joint. Tiffani guides; Jhace follows, learning the rhythm of velding through repetition. His hands still shake, but less. The tremor doesn't vanish, but it becomes... manageable.

At some point, the work shifts. They're not just stabilizing an anchor. They're *learning each other*—how to move in sync, when to push and when to yield, how to trust someone else with weight you've always carried alone.

Tiffani feels it in her chest—not just the rib pain, but something warmer. Trust. Partnership. The beginnings of something she hasn't felt in years.

When the anchor finally stabilizes completely, they sit back, breathing hard, hands still touching the metal.

"You're better at this than you think," Tiffani says quietly.

"I'm slower than you."

"You're *learning*. That's different." She shifts, leaning into him slightly. "Most people can't adapt mid-crisis. You did. That's rare."

Jhace turns his head, meeting her gaze. "Who taught you?"

Tiffani hesitates. She's deflected this question before, but sitting here, exhausted and vulnerable, she can't find the energy to lie.

"Her name was Marisse," Tiffani says quietly. "She was a master velder in Strathmoor. Taught me everything I know about precision and control. Believed that craft should never force—only guide."

"What happened to her?"

"She died holding a transit bridge. Refused to let go even when the vein detonated." Tiffani's voice flattens. "I watched. Couldn't reach her in time. And I've been carrying that failure ever since."

Jhace doesn't say anything. He just reaches out, threading his fingers through hers, gripping tight.

They sit in silence, hands intertwined, sharing the weight of memory and exhaustion.

"Thank you," Jhace says finally.

"For what?"

"For teaching me. For trusting me." He squeezes her hand gently. "For staying."

Tiffani looks at him—scarred hands, exhausted eyes, stubborn refusal to quit—and feels something crack open in her chest. Not breaking. *Opening*.

"I'm not going anywhere," she says.

Jhace smiles—tired, real. "Good."

They stand, dusting off their knees, and walk back toward the storage alcove. The sun is setting. The bells ring sixth hour. The Dregs settle into evening routines—cooking over heat pipes, mending torn fabric, children playing on patched stairs.

The world is still broken. But for now, it holds.

That night, they sit in the alcove, side by side, tools spread between them. Tiffani shows Jhace how to clean velding styli properly—careful, methodical, a ritual of care and maintenance.

Jhace watches her hands move, learning the rhythm. Then he looks up at her face.

"Tiffani."

"Yeah?"

"I don't know how to do this."

"Do what?"

"Be close to someone." He exhales shakily. "I'm not good at it."

Tiffani sets down the stylus, turning to face him fully. "You're doing fine."

"Am I?"

She reaches out, brushing her thumb along his jaw—deliberate, gentle. "You asked for my help. You trusted me with your craft channels. You're learning techniques that require vulnerability." She leans closer. "That's not failing. That's trying."

Jhace catches her wrist, holding her hand against his face. "I don't want to break this."

"You won't."

"How do you know?"

"Because we're both too stubborn to let it collapse." Tiffani smiles. "And because I've seen how you hold things together. You don't quit."

Jhace closes his eyes, breathing slow. Then he pulls her closer, forehead resting against hers.

They sit like that, in the darkness of an alcove that smells like rust and old tools, breathing the same air. No words. Just presence.

Eventually, Tiffani pulls back, just enough to see his face. "Get some rest. Tomorrow we tackle the market dome again. Thirty-one ribs left."

"Thirty-one." Jhace almost laughs. "We'll never finish."

"We will." Tiffani stands, extending her hand. "Together."

Jhace takes her hand, letting her pull him upright. They move deeper into the alcove, finding the shared bedroll. They collapse onto it, exhausted, bodies pressed close in the narrow space.

Tiffani feels Jhace's hand find hers in the dark, fingers threading together.

"Tiffani," he whispers.

"Yeah?"

"Thank you."

She doesn't ask what for. She just squeezes his hand and closes her eyes.

And for the first time in years, neither of them sleeps alone.

---

## CONTINUITY LEDGER — CHAPTER 6

**Posted Clauses Used:**

- Shared vein technique: Tiffani teaches Jhace velding fundamentals, threading resonance through borrowed stylus (consent explicitly given, technique ritualized)
- Velding pedagogy: "Open pathways, don't force"—core principle demonstrated through breath, intention, trust
- Tiffani's teacher revealed: Marisse (master velder, Strathmoor, died holding transit bridge)
- Rib scar visibility: Tiffani shows Jhace the injury, contextualizes cost of precision work
- Heat pipes: Background presence, no investigation (bells mark time passage)

**Artifacts Created/Altered:**

- Borrowed velding stylus: Jhace now possesses tool with pressure glyphs, can perform basic velding
- Northern scaffold anchor: Stabilized through shared vein technique, temporarily safe
- Pedagogical partnership: Tiffani formally teaching Jhace velding—shifts dynamic from equals to mentor/student (adds tension)
- Marisse memorial: Named teacher, establishes Tiffani's lineage and loss

**Costs (Body/Social/Coin):**

- Body: Jhace—hand tremor worsens after 6-hour hold, exhaustion visible
- Body: Tiffani—rib scar flares during shared vein, pain visible and acknowledged
- Emotional: Tiffani reveals teacher's death, vulnerability shown (trust deepens)
- Time: Full afternoon spent teaching, reduces available repair hours

**Time/Bell Markers:**

- Dawn to noon: Jhace braces anchor alone (6 hours, overextension)
- Afternoon: Velding lesson, anchor stabilization
- Sixth bell: Return to alcove, tool maintenance ritual
- Night: Shared rest, hands intertwined

**New Constraint (Persists 2–3 Chapters):**
Jhace is now learning velding—slower, more precise, but less forceful than his natural brasting. This creates tactical *advantage* (can perform delicate repairs previously impossible) but also *limitation* (velding takes longer, can't respond to rapid crises with same speed). In Chapter 7 (The Dome Cracks Wider), this constraint will pressure him: a crisis requires immediate brasting, but Tiffani insists on velding, creating conflict. Additionally, the pedagogical shift (Tiffani as teacher) introduces power imbalance into the partnership—Jhace must reconcile learning from someone he views as equal, while Tiffani must avoid condescension. This tension surfaces in Chapter 7 when Jhace defies her instruction during emergency.

**Romance Micro-Act (Cluster 2, Act 2 of 2):**
Jhace chooses to ask for teaching (vulnerability shown through request for help). Tiffani chooses to reveal Marisse (trust through shared grief). Both choose physical proximity—forehead to forehead, hands intertwined, sleeping together without sexual intent (intimacy as partnership confirmation).

# \# CHAPTER 7 — The Dome Cracks Wider

*Jhace POV*

The glass detonates at second bell.

Jhace is halfway up the dome's eastern rib when he hears it—a sharp *crack*, like bone breaking, followed by the high-frequency shriek of glass fracturing under catastrophic stress. He looks up and sees the stress-trace spider across the crown, branching faster than he can track.

New sabotage.

Someone patterned fresh risers into the dome while they slept. Probably during the night patrol gap. The structure they spent two weeks stabilizing is failing again—worse than before.

"Tiffani!" Jhace shouts.

She's on the opposite side of the dome, thirty meters away, already moving. She sees the fracture pattern and her face goes pale.

"Get down!" she yells. "Now!"

Jhace doesn't move. He presses both hands against the rib beneath him, finding the vein. If he can brace this section—hold it long enough for Tiffani to redistribute the load—maybe they can stop the cascade.

The vein *screams*.

It's worse than the scaffold anchor in Chapter 1. Worse than the stairwell beam in Chapter 5. This isn't structural fatigue—it's active sabotage, designed to cause explosive failure. Whoever patterned these risers knew exactly what they were doing.

Jhace floods the rib with Wholeness—raw, desperate, everything he has. The glass resists. It doesn't want to be healed. It wants to *shatter*.

"Jhace, let go!" Tiffani's voice cuts through the noise. "You can't brast glass under active failure!"

He doesn't let go.

He finds the fracture line and *holds*—forcing the vein to stay intact through sheer refusal to let two hundred people lose their market. The tremor in his hands is back, violent, and his right palm splits open—fresh blood soaking through the bandages.

The rib stabilizes. Barely.

"Now!" Jhace shouts. "Redistribute while I'm holding!"

Tiffani hesitates—just a second, but he sees it. She doesn't think this will work. She thinks he's overextending, killing himself for a repair that's already lost.

But she moves anyway.

She climbs fast, stylus glowing amber in her left hand, and reaches the crown. She presses the stylus against the stress-trace and *guides*—not forcing, not pushing, just opening pathways for the vein to follow.

It's working. The fracture slows. The shriek fades to a low hum.

Then Jhace's grip falters.

His right hand seizes—tendon splits tearing wider, fingers locking into a claw. Pain floods through his arm, white-hot and immediate. He tries to hold, tries to keep the vein stable, but his hand won't *work*.

The rib shudders.

"Jhace!" Tiffani's voice is sharp, panicked.

He lets go.

The vein collapses inward, and the dome *shrieks*—every rib fracturing simultaneously, stress cascading faster than either of them can counter.

Tiffani doesn't hesitate. She throws Form into the crown—not velding, not guiding, just raw structural force—and *locks* the ribs into place with techniques Jhace has never seen.

The dome stops moving.

Every rib freezes mid-fracture, held in suspension by geometry that shouldn't be possible. The stress-traces don't fade—they're still there, visible and lethal—but the structure isn't collapsing.

Not yet.

Jhace stares at Tiffani across the dome. She's breathing hard, face pale, stylus glowing so bright it hurts to look at. Her hands are shaking.

"Where did you learn that?" Jhace asks, voice rough.

Tiffani doesn't answer. She just pulls the stylus back and climbs down, fast and deliberate.

Jhace follows, cradling his right hand against his chest. Blood drips through the bandages, leaving dark spots on cobblestone. By the time he reaches ground level, Tiffani is already kneeling beside a fallen rigger—Kylar, pinned under a beam that sheared free during the cascade.

Kylar's breathing is shallow. There's blood soaking through his shirt. A lot of blood.

Jhace drops to his knees beside them. "Let me—"

"No." Tiffani's voice is flat, controlled. "You're overextended. If you try to heal him now, you'll both collapse."

"I can—"

"You can't." She presses her hands against Kylar's chest, finding the injury. "Broken ribs. Punctured lung. I can stabilize him, but you need to *get back*."

Jhace doesn't move. He watches Tiffani work—precise, methodical, hands glowing faint amber as she threads Form through Kylar's ribs, locking them into place. Not healing. Just... holding. Buying time for the healers to arrive.

It's velding. Advanced velding. Techniques that take years to learn.

Jhace has known Tiffani for three weeks. She told him she learned from her teacher, Marisse, but she never said *how much* she learned. Never showed the full extent of her capacity.

Until now.

Kylar's breathing eases. The blood stops spreading. He's still unconscious, but stable.

Tiffani pulls her hands back, flexing her fingers. Her rib scar is visible through her shirt—flushed, inflamed. She's in pain, but she doesn't show it.

"Who taught you that?" Jhace asks quietly.

Tiffani looks at him. Her eyes are tired. Guarded. "Someone who's gone."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the only one I have." She stands, dusting off her knees. "The healers will be here soon. We need to assess the rest of the dome before—"

"Stop." Jhace pushes himself upright, legs shaking. "Just stop."

Tiffani goes still.

"You've been holding back," Jhace says. "This whole time. You could've fixed the dome yourself. You didn't need me."

"That's not—"

"Then why?" His voice is rougher than intended. "Why hire me? Why teach me? Why let me overextend when you could've just—"

"Because I *can't* do this alone!" Tiffani's voice cracks—just slightly, but enough. "You think I'm holding back? I'm barely holding *together*. Every time I use that technique, my ribs feel like they're splitting open. Every time I push Form past safe limits, I pay for it. And if I try to stabilize an entire dome alone, I'll collapse just like you would."

Jhace stares at her. "Then why didn't you tell me?"

"Because you needed to learn control, not just force. And because—" She exhales shakily. "—because I was scared if I showed you how skilled I really was, you'd stop trusting me. You'd think I was using you."

"Are you?"

Tiffani flinches. "No."

"Then what are you doing?"

She doesn't answer immediately. She just stands there, breathing hard, blood on her hands from Kylar's injury, exhaustion carved into every line of her face.

"I'm trying to survive," she says finally. "And I need you to survive with me."

Jhace doesn't know what to say to that. So he just steps closer, close enough that their shoulders touch.

"Next time," he says quietly, "show me what you can do. All of it. I'd rather know the truth than guess."

Tiffani looks up at him. "Even if the truth is complicated?"

"Especially then."

She almost smiles. "You're stubborn."

"So are you."

They stand in the market square, surrounded by cracked glass and fallen beams, blood on cobblestones and stress-traces glowing faint violet in the early light. The dome holds—barely. Kylar's alive—barely.

Two hundred people will trade beneath this structure tomorrow, and Jhace doesn't know how long it will last.

But for now, it holds.

The healers arrive—three of them, carrying tactile boards and bandage rolls. They take over Kylar's care, murmuring quiet assessments. One healer glances at Jhace's bleeding hand and gestures for him to sit.

He does. Too exhausted to argue.

Tiffani kneels beside him, watching the healer unwrap his bandages. The tendon splits are worse—fresh tears layered over old scars. The healer shakes her head.

"You need to stop using this hand," she says bluntly. "Or you'll lose function permanently."

Jhace doesn't respond. He just stares at his palm—blood and scar tissue, trembling fingers, veins that glow faint from overuse.

Tiffani reaches out, threading her fingers through his left hand. "We'll figure it out."

"How?"

"Together." She squeezes his hand gently. "Same way we've figured out everything else."

Jhace closes his eyes, leaning his head back against the dome's base. He feels her warmth beside him, hears the hum of heat pipes beneath cobblestones, the distant clang of market vendors setting up stalls despite the fracture pattern glowing above them.

The world is broken. But they're still here.

That has to count for something.

The healer finishes wrapping his hand—fresh bandages, thicker than before. She marks something on her tactile board and leaves.

Jhace and Tiffani sit in silence, hands intertwined, watching dawn break over the Dregs.

"We need to find who's sabotaging the dome," Tiffani says quietly.

"I know."

"And we need to finish the repairs before they try again."

"I know."

"And you need to rest."

Jhace almost laughs. "I know."

Tiffani turns her head, meeting his gaze. "I'm sorry I didn't tell you. About my skill level."

"I'm sorry I didn't ask." He squeezes her hand. "I just... assumed you were learning too. Figured we were figuring this out together."

"We are." Tiffani leans closer, resting her forehead against his shoulder. "But I've been doing this longer. And I've lost more than I've saved. That's... hard to explain."

Jhace doesn't push. He just sits there, holding her hand, letting her rest against him.

Eventually, the market opens. Vendors spread their goods beneath cracked glass. Children run through the square, laughing. Mrs. Kettner sets out bread loaves. Chloen, red scarf bright against the morning light, weaves between buyers carrying messages.

The Dregs survive. Because that's what they do.

Jhace and Tiffani stand, dusting off their clothes, and walk back toward the storage alcove. They have work to do—mapping the new sabotage, reinforcing the weakest ribs, teaching other workers how to stabilize structures before the Sanitists shut them down permanently.

But for now, they rest.

That night, they collapse in the alcove, too exhausted for words. Tiffani curls against Jhace's side, her hand resting over his wrapped palm. He feels her breath even out, feels the weight of her trust settling against him.

And for the first time since the dome cracked, he lets himself believe they might actually survive this.

---

## CONTINUITY LEDGER — CHAPTER 7

**Posted Clauses Used:**

- New sabotage attempt: Fresh stress-risers patterned into dome overnight, causing cascade failure
- Jhace overextends: Forces Wholeness into glass, hand seizes, tendon splits worsen (permanent damage escalates)
- Tiffani's advanced Form: Locks entire dome structure mid-collapse using techniques Jhace has never seen (skill reveal)
- Kylar injured: Rigger pinned under beam, punctured lung, stabilized by Tiffani's precision velding
- Heat pipes: Background hum, constant presence, origin uninvestigated

**Artifacts Created/Altered:**

- Dome structure: Partially locked by Tiffani's Form techniques, stress-traces frozen but not healed
- Jhace's right hand: Additional tendon splits, healer warns of permanent function loss if use continues
- Trust fracture: Jhace confronts Tiffani about hidden skill level, vulnerability exchanged (partial repair)
- Sabotage evidence: New patterning confirms coordinated attacks, not random structural failure

**Costs (Body/Social/Coin):**

- Body: Jhace—tendon splits worsen, right hand approaching permanent dysfunction, blood loss
- Body: Tiffani—rib scar flares severely during advanced Form work, chronic pain visible
- Body: Kylar—broken ribs, punctured lung, removed from active repairs (crew resource lost)
- Social: Partnership tension—Jhace questions Tiffani's motives, trust temporarily strained
- Time: Second sabotage forces full dome reassessment, repair timeline extended

**Time/Bell Markers:**

- Second bell: Glass detonation, sabotage cascade begins
- Dawn: Market opens beneath frozen but unstable dome
- Night: Jhace and Tiffani rest in alcove, exhaustion forces pause

**New Constraint (Persists 2–3 Chapters):**
Jhace's right hand is approaching permanent dysfunction—healer explicitly warns further use risks total loss of fine motor control. This forces tactical shift: Jhace must rely more heavily on Tiffani and other workers for delicate repairs, limiting his independent capacity. In Chapter 8 (Storage Room Confession), this constraint pressures their relationship—Jhace must confront his inability to "hold the city alone" and accept collaborative dependency. In Chapter 9 (The Quotas Arrive), the constraint forces Jhace to teach others his techniques, distributing the load across the community. Additionally, Tiffani's skill reveal creates *trust vulnerability*—Jhace now knows she's been holding back, raising questions about her true agenda. This tension surfaces in Chapter 8 (intimate conversation about honesty) and Chapter 9 (Tiffani must prove she's not manipulating him).

**Romance Micro-Act (Cluster 3, Act 1 of 2):**
Jhace chooses to confront Tiffani (vulnerability through honesty demand). Tiffani chooses to admit her fear of rejection (trust through confession). Both choose physical proximity after conflict—forehead to shoulder, hands intertwined, sleeping together as reconciliation.
